Technical University of Denmark as the Ô¨Ånal project of the Master of Science in Civil Engineering.
The project was submitted on January 22nd 2017 for 30 ECTS points.
A special thanks to supervisor Professor Kristian Dahl Hertz of the Department of Civil Engineer-
ing, DTU for his keen supervision and guidance through the project. Thank you for always letting
your door being open for us to come and ask our questions.
We would also like to thank Morten S. Rasmussen, CTO of Abeo for his guidance regarding the
design of the mould and other general questions about the SL-deck.

Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
Abstract
The project focuses on optimization of the construction of multi-story buildings by using a super-
light multi-story car park as an example.
The idea is to decrease the construction height by
the use of a blade connection between the decks and thereby exclude beams in the construction.
Furthermore to use SL-decks to increase the Ô¨Åre resistance and reduce construction weight of the
building, hence super-light.

Technical University of Denmark

1
Introduction
The purpose of this thesis is to optimize the way of constructing multi-story buildings. A super-
light multi-story car park has been designed in this thesis to proof that that the systems and
methods used are applicable, and that they will be applicable for multi-story constructions in
general.
A multi-story car park are constructed traditionally by the use of columns, beams and deck ele-
ments. The columns and beams can be made of concrete or steel and the deck elements are usually
hollow core slabs or solid concrete slabs. The super-light multi-story car park is compared with
an existing multi-story car park located at Bispebjerg Hospital, Copenhagen Denmark, which is
constructed traditionally. The super-light multi-story car park is made with roughly the same
dimensions as the one in Bispebjerg, which makes the building eÔ¨Äectiveness comparable.
The idea is to make a Ô¨Åre proof, a more eÔ¨Äective and a more Ô¨Çexible multi-story car park. The
super-light multi-story car park is made without beams, but instead uses a blade connection
between the deck elements by having a Gerber-system and using the SL-deck (Super-Light-deck).
The Gerber-system is a Gerber-girder made of solid concrete with Ô¨Çanges on the sides, for the
SL-decks to be supported by. The Gerber-girder will span between the columns, and the SL-decks
will span between the Gerber-girders.
The SL-deck has been proven by full scale test to be very Ô¨Åre resistant, and is a solid element,
which makes it more Ô¨Çexible in terms of form, connections and reinforcement compared to a hollow
core slab for instance. The Ô¨Çexibility of the super-light multi-story car park mainly consists of the
Ô¨Çexibility of span lengths. The span lengths of the Gerber-girder and SL-decks can be varied after
need, and has proven to be limited mostly due to vibrations.
The optimization of this project is to make sure that the blade connection works and is reinforced
in a way so the load is transferred as desired. Using prefabricated elements exclusively and by
that simplifying the production of concrete elements with Ô¨Çanges and to increase the span lengths
as much as possible as long as the vibrations allow it.
8
==PAGE:10==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
2
Prerequisites for the multi-story car park
The necessary dimensions and demands for a multi-story car park is investigated before the design
process begins. The dimensions of the cars are taken into consideration and their turning areas
has to be large enough to make the driver feel comfortable. The prerequisites of the multi-story
car park has been investigated in a spacial course at the Technical University of Denmark, the
work of that special course can be seen in [14].
The multi-story car park is constructed as a composition of SL-decks and a Gerber system. The
system is constructed without the use of beams, which lowers the construction height. The system
uses a low amount of columns and has long spans, which makes the design more Ô¨Çexible.
The prerequisites of the multi-story car park are described in the following section.
2.1
Layout and dimensions of parking areas
The dimensions of the parking areas have to correspond to the sizes of the vehicles in the country
where the given project is constructed. The Danish Road Directory recommends standard dimen-
sions of a car as seen in Figure 2.1. According to Danish Road Directory, a parking space with
suÔ¨Écient dimensions has a width of 2.5 m and length of 5.0 m, [6]. The free height in a multi-story
car park is recommended to 2.3 m with a car height limit of 2.1 m.
Figure 2.1: Car dimensions according to the Danish Road Directory, [15].
The arrangement of the parking spaces has multiple options. The most commonly used are the
90¬∞ and 45¬∞ angled parking spaces, see Figure 2.2. The 90¬∞ parking spaces have less area waste
compared to the 45¬∞ parking spaces, since they do not have wasted area in the end of the parking
space as seen in Figure 2.2 b. However the 45¬∞ is more accessible and comfortable to park in. Using
a layout with 90¬∞ parking space requires a larger manoeuvre area which results in a lane width of
6.5 m compared to a lane width of 3.5 m for a layout with 45¬∞ parking space. The layout of the
multi-story car park is presented in appendix A for 90¬∞ and appendix B for 45¬∞ parking spaces.
The layout for the 90¬∞ parking spaces have a Ô¨Çoor area of 2094 m2 with 74 parking spaces. The

layout for the 45¬∞ parking spaces have a Ô¨Çoor area of 2167 m2 with 71 parking spaces. The layout
with 45¬∞ parking spaces has been chosen as the layout of interest in this project due to the comfort
of the 45¬∞ parking spaces even though the Ô¨Çoor area is a bit larger. The Ô¨Çoor area is approximately
140 m2 larger than the Ô¨Çoor area of Bispebjerg Hospitals multi-story car park which has a Ô¨Çoor
area of approximately 2025 m2 with 70 parking spaces.

2.2
Stairs
The design of the stairs has to comply with the rules listed in the building regulations, which take
the requirements for the type of people using the building into consideration. The regulations for
stairs are stated in [5].
When designing stairs for public areas, the steps of the stairs has to be the right size in order for
people to feel comfortable. A inclined step length of around 63 cm is standard for Danish people,
the Danish regulations state that: 2 x Step height + step depth = 61 - 65 cm, preferable 63 cm,
with a maximum step height of 18 cm and minimum step depth of 28 cm. By fulÔ¨Ålling this rule a
comfortable inclination of the stairs are obtained.
The width of the stairs has to comply with the space for two people passing each other and for a
helper to help a walking-impaired up or down the stairs. A minimum width of 1.25 m free space
between the railing is required. A width of 1.5 m is constructed to make room for the railings. A
free space of 1 m is needed in front of doors and the minimum ceiling height has to be 2.1 m.
The construction height of the building is 2.65 m, where 2.33 m are free space, which includes free
space for joints. and 0.32 m are the deck height. The amount of steps can then be calculated by
using the earlier stated requirements for steps:

length of the stair and gives the physically disabled a resting spot. A stair length of 2.1 m is then
used with a 1.5 m wide platform as shown in Figure 2.4 a. The total length of the stairs including

2.3
Elevator
Elevators are necessary in public buildings due to accessibility of people in wheelchairs. In order
for the elevators to have enough room for the wheelchairs, a minimum width of 110 cm and a
length of 140 cm has to be fulÔ¨Ålled with a door width of minimum 0.8 m. The elevator is sketched
in Figure 2.5.

2.4
Load from the cars
The mass of cars has been investigated in [14]. The mass of cars was evaluated due to statistics
from 2016 of the amount of cars which was registered in various mass classes. A tendency of
increasing amount of heavy cars was noticed and a 80 % -quantile was concluded as the best
option for the mass of cars. Furthermore 125 kg was added to the 80 % -quantile in order to take
driver and fuel into account. This resulted in a total mass of 1500 kg.
The load of the cars have been calculated for the deck width and for the horizontal length of two
45¬∞ parking spaces and lane, resulting in a width of 2.4 m and a length of 14.1 m. The lane also
functions as a manoeuvre area. The load is calculated accounting for the weight of 3 cars, 1 in
each parking space and one manoeuvring in the lane. See Figure 2.6.

When cars brake a horizontal load occur, which has to be taken by the decks and connections in
the structure. The braking force is therefore calculated for at standard car with a mass of 1500 kg.
The maximum speed in the multi-story car park is U = 30 km/h, which correspond to 8.33 m/s

2.5
Structural Ô¨Åre safety of a multi-story car park
The structural Ô¨Åre safety of a multi-story car park is determined based on the assumption that the
Ô¨Åre will be able to spread to all cars in the case that the multi-story car park is full. The Ô¨Åre load
has been concluded and discussed in [14] and is concluded to 300 MJ/m2 for a fully developed Ô¨Åre.
The structural Ô¨Åre safety is an important topic, because it has been a problem in the past due to
the fact that the Ô¨Åre load assumption have been based on full scale tests made in 1995, which is
diÔ¨Äerent from today since the materials used to build a car has changed. Plastic fuel containers
and more light metals which ignites and contributes to faster Ô¨Åre spreading is more commonly
used today in cars. The full scale tests concluded that Ô¨Åre would not spread to more than 4-5 cars,
which is unrealistic.
A multi-story car park made of a majority of SL-decks is a solution to the structural Ô¨Åre safety,
since the SL-decks has been tested to be able to withstand more than 4 hours of Ô¨Åre. The remaining
structural elements are made in solid concrete and have to be checked if they are able to withstand a
standard Ô¨Åre of 120 minutes. It is also recommended that the construction elements can withstand
a fully developed Ô¨Åre with the concluded Ô¨Åre load of 300 MJ/m2.

3.3
Punching
This section will introduce the theory for punching, which is very relevant when concrete decks
are exposed to concentrated loads, which in this case is the supporting columns. This section is
based on the method described in [4], which is based on DS/EN 1992-1-1.
3.3.1
Primary control perimeter
The principle of punching is based on a control by the edge of the load and by a primary control
perimeter which is 2d from the edge of the loaded area. Where d is the average eÔ¨Äective height
from the reinforcement center in the top of the deck to the bottom of the deck. The primary
control perimeter is calculated as always being as close to the edge as possible, but with a distance
of 2d. See Figure 3.1 for examples.
Figure 3.1: Examples of control perimeters.
20
==PAGE:22==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
The circular and rectangular columns are the most common. The primary control perimeter of
the circular column cross section with diameter D is deÔ¨Åned by:
u1 = œÄ(D + 4d)
(3.36)
And the primary control perimeter of the rectangular column cross section is deÔ¨Åned by:
u1 = 2(b + h + 2œÄd)
(3.37)
More rules apply to the determination of the primary control perimeter if there is a hole close to
the perimeter or if the column is placed near a free edge. Since there is no decks with holes near
a column in this project, this subject will not be addressed, however there will be columns near a
free edge. When determining the primary control perimeter near a free edge, the control perimeter
is running to the free edge, which will cut it oÔ¨Ä. Examples can be see in Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Examples of control perimeters near edge.
The shear stress based on the design load can now be determined by knowing the control perimeter,
u1 and the depth of the deck, d.
ved = Ved
u1d
(3.38)

achievable load capacity for punching, even if reinforcement is considered.

3.3.3
Load capacity with reinforcement
There are ways to increase the punching capacity if the concrete punching capacity explained in
the last section did not prove suÔ¨Écient. This could be done by using reinforcement, however,
punching shear reinforcement is diÔ¨Écult to use.
22
==PAGE:24==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
The eÔ¨Äect of the reinforcement is accounted for by taking a part of the load capacity without
reinforcement and then add the contribution from the reinforcement.
An example of punching reinforcement can be seen in Figure 3.3. This reinforcement set-up can
only be used if the amount of vertical reinforcement bars are kept to a minimum, which in most
cases are suÔ¨Écient. It is important that the reinforcement is anchored. Furthermore, the Ô¨Årst row
of vertical reinforcement have to be placed 0.3d‚àí0.5d from the edge of the column according to [4].
Figure 3.3: Example of placement of punching reinforcement.
The design punching capacity can be calculated for the reinforcement setup with one row of
reinforcement by:
vRd,cs = 0.75vRd,c + Aswfywd,ef
u1d
(3.47)
where Asw is the cross section of the Ô¨Årst row of vertical reinforcement bars. fywd,ef is the eÔ¨Äective
design strength of the punching reinforcement. This is given by:
fywd,ef = 250 + 0.25d ‚â§fywd
(3.48)
where d is in mm. The reason behind this reduction is that the reinforcement needs a certain length
in order to receive the force, which makes it dependant on d. Equation 3.47 is based on many
tests with diÔ¨Äerent reinforcement set-ups. This is thereby an empirical equation which should be
on the safe side to use even if the reinforcement set-up does not look like Figure 3.3 because of the
reduction of the yield strength of the punching reinforcement.
It should be noted that an additional analysis is needed when using punching reinforcement. The
punching capacity should be investigated with the control perimeter moved to start at the vertical
reinforcement bars, since the punching failure could happen outside of the vertical reinforcement
bars.

4
Construction analysis and argumentation
This section will present the dimensions and layout of the multi-story car park as well as the load
combinations and all the results regarding dimensioning of the construction elements. Furthermore,
the connections between the construction elements and an idea to a simple and eÔ¨Äective mould for
production of deck with Ô¨Çanges, are presented.
4.1
Dimensions of the multi-story car park
The dimensions of the super-light multi-story car park has been chosen based on the dimensions of
the newly constructed multi-story car park at Bispebjerg Hospital.The layout of the parking spaces
have been chosen in order to have as eÔ¨Äective a building as possible with dimensions comparable
with Bispebjerg Hospital. The drawings of the super-light multi-story car park with dimensions
can be seen in appendix C.
The layout for the super-light multi-story car park was chosen as 45¬∞ parking spaces. The layout
has a Ô¨Çoor area of 2167 m2 with 71 parking spaces on each Ô¨Çoor, see appendix C. The layout has
4 handicap spaces on Ô¨Çoor 1-7 due the requirement of having 1-2 handicap parking spaces every
25 regular parking spaces. Since there is 71 parking spaces on each Ô¨Çoor, atleast 3 of them have to
be handicap parking spaces. The building has 9 Ô¨Çoors, which means that 27 handicap spaces are
required in the building. This is fulÔ¨Ålled if there is 4 handicap spaces on Ô¨Çoor 1-7, which results
in 28 handicap spaces in total. The handicap parking spaces could be placed on the Ô¨Årst 5 Ô¨Çoors
of the building, however since two elevators are present in the building, travelling between stories
should not be a problem, even with a wheelchair. The dimensions of the handicap parking spaces
is the reason why all handicap spaces can not be placed at the Ô¨Årst 2 Ô¨Çoors, but have to be spread
on multiple Ô¨Çoors in order not to reduce the amount of parking spaces due to the layout.
The layout of the super-light multi-story car park has resulted in the placement of the columns,
which dictates the span lengths of the Gerber-girders and the SL-decks. The span lengths used
for the multi-story car park can be seen in Table 4.1.
Table 4.1: Span lengths of the deck elements.

super-light multi-story car park will have one more Ô¨Çoor of parking spaces than the existing multi-
story car park at Bispebjerg Hospital with approximately the same total height. A sketch of the
Gerber-system can be seen in Figure 4.1.

4.2
Load combinations
The design load combinations are calculated as shown in [12]. The considered load combinations
are:
¬à Dominating snow load
¬à Dominating live load
¬à Dominating wind load
All load combinations are made with consequence class CC3, which results in a KFI = 1.1 since
the building is taller than 12 m.

include both live load and snow load since there will be parking spaces on the roof. The vertical
load is calculated as a surface load and is calculated by:

4.3
Dimensioning of the Gerber-girder
The Gerber-girder is used to minimize the construction height of the building, since beams will
be removed and the decks will span between the Gerber-girders sides. The Gerber-girder consist
of three parts, see Figure 4.4, two plates which is Ô¨Åxed with the columns and one Ô¨Çoating deck
between the two plates. The Ô¨Çoating deck is simply supported by the two plates, the support is
done by making Ô¨Çanges of half the deck thickness and utilizing the Ô¨Çanges for a blade connection
between the Ô¨Çoating deck and the plates.
The SL-deck is supported the same way with Ô¨Çanges transverse to the span direction of the Ô¨Çoating
deck. Drawings of the Gerber-girder can be seen in appendix C.
Figure 4.4: Sketch of the Gerber-girder.
The setup of the Gerber-girder can be considered as a static system as shown in Figure 4.5 a. The
columns are simply supported in the bottom and Ô¨Åxed in the top with the plate deck. The plate
deck spans 1.2 m to each side. This setep results in positive moment in the Ô¨Çoating deck and
negative moment in the plate element, the moment distribution is shown in Figure 4.5 b. The
Gerber-girder allows the engineer to variate the maximum moment by changing the zero point
connection distance from the column, and thereby choosing the span of the Ô¨Çoating deck and the
plate element to beneÔ¨Åt the situation mostly.

4.3.1
The plate element
The plate element can be seen on Figure 4.6 with the column. The plate is made of high strength
concrete, C55, with reinforcement in the top against negative moment and shear reinforcement
in the Ô¨Çanges and the web. All calculations of the plate element can be found in appendix E.1,
appendix E.2 and appendix E.3.
Figure 4.6: Sketch of a column with the plate element.
The cross section of the column and the plate is double symmetrical as shown in Figure 4.7 a. The
plate is rectangular with dimensions of 2.4 m x 2.4 m and has a thickness of 0.32 m. The Ô¨Çanges
of the plate are 0.16 m high and have a depth of 0.16 m + 0.05 m to make room for the joint
casting, as shown in Figure 4.7 b. The height of the column is 2.33 m with rectangular dimensions
as dimensioned in section 4.5.

The resulting design of the reinforcement is done as shown in Figure 4.10 for the longitudinal

44
==PAGE:46==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
shear forces and negative moment from the reaction on the Ô¨Çange and the surface load on the plate.
The negative moment in the cross section is taken by tension in the reinforcement bars placed in
the top of the plate in both directions. The shear force is trying to make the cross section break
outwards by pressing the Ô¨Çange down, which creates a tension force in the horizontal and the
sloped stirrup. The web stirrup and the concrete in the bottom is in compression.
For the longitudinal direction, the forces are transferred further into the cross section where the
web stirrup is in tension and the concrete has a sloped compression strut. The stirrups are made
with three widths to distribute the shear forces more equally through the web because of the
large width of the cross section, see Figure 4.10. For the transverse direction, the shear forces are
transferred from the Ô¨Çange into the concrete which is strong enough to withstand the shear forces,
see Figure 4.11.

4.3.2
The Ô¨Çoating deck
The Ô¨Çoating deck is a simply supported deck spanning between the plate elements. The element
is made of high strength concrete C55, with Ô¨Çanges in the ends and the Ô¨Çanges to support the
SL-deck along the longitudinal sides, as shown in Figure 4.12. The deck is reinforced in the bottom
with pre-stressed wires and transverse reinforcement in the top. All calculations for the Ô¨Çoating
deck can be found in appendix F.1.
Figure 4.12: Sketch of the Ô¨Çoating deck.
The Ô¨Çoating deck has a span of 6.025 m and a with of 2.4 m. The deck height is 0.32 m, with
Ô¨Çanges of 0.16 m in height and a depth of 0.16 m + 0.05 m to make room for joint casting, as
shown in Figure 4.13 b. The deck is loaded with a surface load on the web and a line load on each
supporting Ô¨Çange from the SL-decks, as shown in Figure 4.13. The deck has upper Ô¨Çanges at the
longitudinal ends which is simply supported by the plate elements.

The design of the reinforcement in the Ô¨Çoating deck is done as shown in Figure 4.17 for the
longitudinal direction and in Figure 4.18 for the transverse direction. The forces acting in the
longitudinal directions introduces a positive moment from the SL-decks supported by the Ô¨Çoating
deck and the surface load acting on the Ô¨Çoating deck. A negative moment is acting in the transverse
direction. The cross section for the longitudinal direction has compression in the concrete in the
top and the horizontal and sloped bar in the Ô¨Çange is in tension, as shown in Figure 4.17. An
angled compression strut occurs in the web, which causes tension in the vertical bars of the vertical
stirrups. The transverse direction is aÔ¨Äected by compression in the bottom of the cross section
and tension in the horizontal and the angled stirrup. The shear forces are transferred into the
web, where the concrete forms an inclined compression strut, which causes tension in the vertical
stirrups, see Figure 4.18.

4.4
The SL-deck
The SL-Deck will be spanning between the Gerber-girders as a simply supported deck, similar to
the Ô¨Çoating deck, see Figure 4.19. The blade connection between the SL-deck and Gerber-girder
is assumed simply supported and will therefore not transfer any moment. The static system will
therefore look as shown in Figure 4.5.
Figure 4.19: The blade connection system including three SL-Decks spanning between two Gerber-
girders.
4.4.1
Dimensioning of the SL-deck
The SL-decks are dimensioned by assuming a simply supported beam moment and shear distribu-
tion. The moment capacity of the cross section is calculated and the cross section is reinforced to
be able to carry the maximum moment. The maximum shear force will be the reaction acting on
50
==PAGE:52==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
the Ô¨Çanges of the Gerber-girder, therefore the same shear reinforcement is used in the Ô¨Çanges of
the SL-deck as in the Gerber-girder.
The maximum moment as well as the moment capacity of the cross section is shown in Table 4.16.
The calculations can be seen in appendix G.1. Furthermore, the cross section analysis has been
done by a program made by Professor Kristian Hertz, after the moment capacity in appendix G.1
was calculated to check if the same results were found for the moment capacity. The cross section
analysis made with the program can be found in appendix G.2 . The approximate same results
were found, and the program was used for the dimensioning of top reinforcement of the SL-deck
and it was used to Ô¨Ånd the mass and the stiÔ¨Äness of the SL-deck.

4.4.2
Vibration analysis of the SL-deck
The vibration comfort of the SL-deck is investigated due to long spans. The comfort criteria is
based on recommendation from [8]. The recommendation in [8] is for buildings housing people
like oÔ¨Éce buildings or residential buildings. The comfort criteria for oÔ¨Éce buildings or residential
buildings are a minimum of 5 Hz for the lowest acceptable eigenfrequency. The amount of person
traÔ¨Éc in a multi-story car park is limited, since the users of the building will not stay in the
building over a long period of time. The assumed acceptable frequency is 4-5 Hz with preferably
5 Hz.
The eigenfrequency is calculated for the Ô¨Årst mode shape for a beam, since the lowest eigenfre-
quency is wanted, by the use of section 3.1.
The SL-deck has a thickness of t = 320 mm and is spanning L = 11.7 m. The eigenfrequency is
calculated for a single deck element and results in:
f = 3.77 Hz
The eigenfrequency is also calculated with the assumption of a very wide deck element, thereby
taking the stiÔ¨Äness contribution from neighbouring deck elements into account. This is done by
increasing the stiÔ¨Äness by 50 %. The eigenfrequency of the decks spanning between Gerber-girders
are calculated to:
f = 4.62 Hz
The calculations can be seen in appendix G.3. The eigenfrequency is found without including
53
==PAGE:55==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
two parked cars on each side of the deck in the self-weight. The contribution of the cars to the
eigenfrequency is investigated. This is done with a FEM model made in Abaqus.
The model is initially veriÔ¨Åed by comparing the result of the Ô¨Årst mode shape, with the analytical
result. The Ô¨Årst mode shape found in Abaqus can be seen in appendix G.4. A convergence analysis
has been made to conclude the needed mesh to get a converged result, see Figure 4.22.
Figure 4.22: Convergence plot of Abaqus results.
The Abaqus model resulted in a frequency of 3.75 Hz which is very close to the analytical result.
The model is thereby used to get a result of the eigenfrequency with a car in each end of a single
deck element.
The eigenfrequency of a single deck element with a car in each end is investigated by using the
Abaqus model to calculate the eigenfrequency. The resulting frequency is expected to be lower
than without the contribution from the cars, since the mass has increased. The load scenario with
the cars included is shown in Figure 4.23.
Pcar
Pcar
Pdeck
Figure 4.23: Load scenario with cars.
This load scenario is implemented in the Abaqus model, which resulted in a eigenfrequency of
f = 3.34 Hz
54
==PAGE:56==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
for a single deck element. The mass of the cars is also assumed evenly distributed over the span.
The eigenfrequency of a single element will then be:
f = 3.17 Hz
It is seen that by using the placement of the cars, a higher eigenfrequency is achieved compared to
the case of evenly distributed mass. These results are for a single deck element and has therefore
no stiÔ¨Äness contribution from neighbouring deck elements taken into account. This was attempted
in Abaqus by increasing the elastic modulus of the materials by 50 %, supporting the deck at sides
as it was very wide, or simply increase the width of the deck. All attempts ended in unrealistic
results compared to earlier achieved results as well as analytical results. However, if the frequency
is calculated with the mass of the cars evenly distributed and with the contribution from the
neighbouring deck elements included, the frequency will be:
f = 3.89 Hz
If the cars are placed in the ends, the frequency will be assumed greater than 4 Hz, since the
diÔ¨Äerence between the single element results shows a higher frequency when the cars are placed at
the ends. It is also an option to make tests to determine and proof if the frequency is suÔ¨Écient.
If the frequency needs to be increased, it can be done by reducing the span length of the deck
elements or installing dampers. Reduction of span length will result in a reduced Ô¨Çoor area or
an extra row of columns, which would either increase the construction cost or reduce the amount
of parking spaces on each Ô¨Çoor. If dampers were to be installed, it would result in an increase
in the construction cost. Therefore it is assumed that the choice would be to accept the current
eigenfrequency, since the building is used as a multi-story car park which means that the building
will not occupy people for long periods of time. It is noted that the vibration requirements are a
comfort criteria, the frequency of the SL-decks are thereby accepted based on the earlier mentioned
arguments. The criteria was assumed acceptable at 4 Hz, which is assumed achieved.

Dimensioning of the columns
Columns are the supporting part in the Gerber-system, as shown in Figure 4.5 a. The column is
Ô¨Åxed with the plate part and simply supported in the bottom as shown in Figure 4.24 a. A linear
moment distribution is present in the column as shown inFigure 4.24 b if the plate is aÔ¨Äected by
diÔ¨Äerent loads, e.g. by a parked car at one side and not on the other.

2.33m
(a) Static system of column.
(b) Moment in column.
Figure 4.24: Static view of column.
The size of the moment in the column is dependent on which column is investigated and how the
decks are loaded. In Figure 4.25, the three types of load scenarios for the columns are shown. The
column placement is shown in appendix H. The inner column in Figure 4.25 a is loaded without
any moment but with a central normal force when the decks around is fully loaded. A moment
is present in the column when one side is fully loaded and the other side is empty of cars. The
column has to be able to withstand the forces from both the normal force when fully loaded and
the moment when unevenly loaded.
The edge column B has the same length of Ô¨Çoating decks spanning on each side, but only SL-decks
on one side. The largest forces in the column occurs when the SL-decks are fully loaded, which
results in a moment and a normal force in the column.
For edge column D, in Figure 4.25 b, the SL-decks are spanning symmetrically on both sides and
the Gerber-girder only on one side. The largest moment is in column D, which is only loaded at
one side. Biaxial moment can occur in edge column D, when only one side of the SL-decks has
live load acting on the decks. The column has to be able to withstand both moments and normal
force for all these loading cases.

4.6
Wind bracing system
4.6.1
Facade
The facade consist of parapets on the edge of the decks.
The parapets will work as a crash
barrier and as a closure of the structure. The parapets have a height of 1.2 m and can be seen
on Figure 4.27. The parapets are used to counterweight some of the moment in the outer edge
columns, which is the reason of a wall thickness of 300 mm.

s112846 & s112880

The parapets are used to transfer the wind loads into the decks. The parapets also have to be
able to withstand a collision with a car. The dimensioning are done for the wind and the collision
separately, because they are acting opposite of each other, which means the worst case is when
one load is acting. The load case for wind is shown in Figure 4.28, with a surface load from the
wind. The largest moment is at the bottom, because the parapet is Ô¨Åxed at the bottom and free
in the top. A parabolic moment and a linear shear force is acting in the wall.

4.6.2
Disc eÔ¨Äect
The decks are transferring the wind load to the cores by the disc eÔ¨Äect in the decks. It is necessary
to check if the decks and joints between the decks can withstand the transferred forces to account
for the disc eÔ¨Äect. The angle of the forces is varying depending of the location of the force on the
facade, see Figure 4.32 a. The forces move through the decks in an angle, split into two components,
which results in two reaction forces in the joints between the decks. The reaction forces are tension
forces in the joints, as shown in Figure 4.32 b.
63
==PAGE:65==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
pwind
(a) Movement of forces in the decks.
(b) Forces in joint.
Figure 4.32: Illustation of disc eÔ¨Äect.
Tension reinforcement is necessary in the joint between two decks in order to withstand the tension
forces. When the wind load acts perpendicular to the core, the largest tension force is in the
longitudinal joint. The tension force in the longitudinal joint decreases, when the angle of attack
of the force increases and thereby increases the tension force in the transverse joint. The joint
reinforcement is calculated to withstand the maximum tension force acting in the deck, which is
in the bottom of the building. The tension force in the joints are calculated by distributing the
shear force in the bottom of the building on half of the width of the building, and multiplying that
line load by the width of one deck in order to Ô¨Ånd the tension force in each joint.

The core walls are prefabricated walls made in a height of 2.33 m. The core walls are connected

tension wall is the only wall needing longitudinal reinforcement.
The reinforcement of the tension wall has been chosen to be used in all the walls, due to the fact
that the wind could hit from another direction and create tension in another wall. Furthermore,
it has been chosen to use the same walls for all Ô¨Çoors through up the building. The walls could
be redesigned for each Ô¨Çoor, but having the same wall through the building will ease the work on
site and reduce the risk of misplaced walls.

4.7
Dimensioning of the access ramp
The access ramps are an essential part of the super-light multi-story car park which has to be
dimensioned to be comfortable for the drivers.
The ramp is constructed quadratic rather than circular, because the radius of a circular ramp
would get to large. A large radius would construct an unnecessary long access ramp, which would
use a higher amount of material than the quadratic ramp. The ramp can be seen on Figure 4.35.
Figure 4.35: 3D view of access ramps.
In order to determine the slope of the ramp, section 2.1 is used, where the maximum slopes is
stated. The outer ramp is used for traÔ¨Éc driving up, therefore, a soft start and ending with a
slope of a/2 is used. The inner ramp is used for traÔ¨Éc going down. This ramp is made with one
constant slope. This design is chosen because the slope should not exceed 10 %. The length and
height of the inner ramp just fulÔ¨Ål this requirement. The Ô¨Ånal dimensions and slopes are seen in
Figure 4.36.
Figure 4.36: Cross section of ramp.
The ramps provides the driver with good space to manoeuvre and a minimal number of turns. All
calculations for the access ramps can be seen in appendix K.1. Drawings on the ramps can be
found in the plan and section drawings in appendix C.
68
==PAGE:70==

4.7.1

The dimensioning of the ramp decks are done using the same theory as for the SL-decks. The load
acting on the deck is the same as for the SL-decks at the roof. The ramp consist of two horizontal
platforms at the ends and sloped decks in between. All decks has a thickness of 320 mm and a
minimum width of 3.7 m, leaving space for a minimum lane width of 3.3 m and two parapets. The
sloped decks are simply supported in both ends by beams and the horizontal platforms are simply
supported by a beam in one end and by the Gerber-girder in the other end. The Ô¨Ånal capacity
and reinforcement for each section of the ramp is calculated and can be seen in Table 4.30. The

4.8
Connection details
This section will show and describe the connections between the various construction elements.
The drawings of the connections can be found in appendix C.
4.8.1
Wall-Deck-Wall connection
The core is constructed by prefabricated wall elements, connected by a Ô¨Åxed connection, shown in
Figure 4.40. The connection consist of three elements, two walls and one deck connected with a
Peikko SUMO 39P wall shoe. A PPM 39 anchorage bolt is placed in the top of the wall elements,
going through the deck element and fastened with a nut and a AL 39 washer in the wall shoe.
Furthermore, the connection is aÔ¨Ñicted by a negative moment from the deck.
The deck and
wall is also connected by having the reinforcement bar in tension, going through the deck and
fastened with a nut, to account for the negative moment. The forces in the connection are shown
in Figure 4.40 b. The SUMO 39P wall shoe has a tension capacity of 500 kN according to [1],
which means that 3 wall shoes per meter wall is needed to withstand the tension of the wind.
The construction phase is executed in 4 steps. First, the wall is placed and the wall shoe is fastened.
Secondly, the deck is placed on top of the wall and the nut for the reinforcement bar is fastened.
Thirdly, the joint is cast and Ô¨Ånally the next wall is placed on top of the deck and the wall shoe
is fastened.
(a) Detail of the connection.
(b) Forces acting in the connection
Figure 4.40: Core wall - deck connection.
73
==PAGE:75==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
4.8.2
Column-Deck-Column connection
The prefabricated columns are produced with a Peikko SUMO 16H wall shoe, see [1]. The columns
are produced with a single shoe in the center, to create a simple support for the bottom of
the column. The wall shoe is not taking any tension or shear forces since the columns are in
compression. The purpose of the wall shoe is only to create the connection. The shear forces in
the connection is taken by the concrete in the joint, see Table 4.36, calculations can be seen in
appendix L.1. The concrete used for the joints have to be C55 in order to be able to withstand
the normal force in the bottom of the building. This concrete has been chosen to be used for all
the column joints in the building. All columns has a 30 mm cast joint in the bottom. HPM 16
anchor bolts, [1], are placed in the top of the columns, with a length of 2 m. AL 16 washers, [1],
are used to fasten the connection.
The construction of the columns are done by placing the lower column and fasten it. The deck is
placed and the through-going reinforcement bars are fastened with the nuts and the joint is cast.
The upper column is placed and the nut in the wall shoe is fastened to the anchor with a ratchet
wrench. The hole in the column due to the wall shoe is cast when the wall shoe is fastened.

4.9
Structural Ô¨Åre safety
The super-light multi-story car park is required to be able to withstand 120 minutes of standard
Ô¨Åre.
The Ô¨Åre load is assumed as 300 MJ/m2 in case of fully developed Ô¨Åre.
Both cases are
considered, where the fully developed Ô¨Åre will not be a requirement, but a recommendation. The
Ô¨Åre resistance of the Gerber-girder, SL-deck, smallest column and core wall is calculated. This
is done for both bending and shear for the Gerber-girder, bending for the SL-decks and for a
reinforced column and wall exposed to 120 minutes of standard Ô¨Åre and a fully developed Ô¨Åre with
a Ô¨Åre load of 300 MJ/m2.
4.9.1

cases. The temperature of the reinforcement in the bottom of the Ô¨Çoating beam as well as the
average deterioration factor, ŒæcM and the stress distribution factor, Œ∑, has been calculated in
the program made by Kristian Hertz called ConFire, see appendix M.2. The temperatures were
calculated to:
TFDF = 321¬∞C
TSF,120 = 275¬∞C
It is noted that the temperature of the fully developed Ô¨Åre is in the hot condition which happens
after 203 minutes for the bottom pre-stressing wires.
The temperature of the shear reinforcement in the bottom as well as ŒæcM and Œ∑ is calculated, also
in ConFire, see appendix M.2.
Tst,FDF = 413¬∞C
Tst,SF,120 = 402¬∞C

4.11
Innovation of mould
In-situ casting is a time demanding and money consuming process, which companies wish to
minimize. Casting the elements at a factory is easier, has fewer working hours and better working
space, which lowers the expenses. But for the multi-story car park, elements with Ô¨Çanges are
used, which complicates the casting process. Foam is currently used to shape the Ô¨Çanges at the
factory, but foam is time demanding to shape. The time demanding process is one reason why
blade connections are not widely used. A new type of mould is innovated for production of the
elements. The mould is seen in Figure 4.53.
Figure 4.53: 3D perspective of the mould.
The mould, designed for the production consist of two parts. The upper part, see Figure 4.54 a,
which keeps the shape of the Ô¨Çanges. The upper part is a folded metal plate. The upper part
is folded two times and holes for the pre-stressing wires are cut in the plate. The mould will
therefore be able to be placed after the wires have been pre-stressed. The part is designed as a
thin steel plate which is attached as a permanent part of the deck. The plate can act as contact
surface between the connected elements. The lower part of the mould, see Figure 4.54 a, is made
to support the Ô¨Çange during the casting. The lower part is magnetic which makes it easier for
the two parts to keep in place. The lower part has holes for the pre-stressing wires and has four
supports at the back for the outer edge of the upper mould, to prevent tipping when the concrete
is poured into the mould.
The pre-stressing wires are set in place and tensioned. The upper part is placed together with the
reinforcement. The lower part of the mould is placed at the end of the decks and the casting of
the concrete can start. After the casting has ended, the reinforcement lines are cut and the decks
87
==PAGE:89==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
are lifted out of the mould, leaving the lower part which can be reused.
When using the mould for the Ô¨Çanges on the side of the Gerber-girder, the mould is used in the
same way, but without the holes since no pre-stressing wires are placed in this direction. The the
lower part is excluded, since the Ô¨Çange is placed in the bottom half of the cross section.
(a) Upper part.
(b) Lower part.
Figure 4.54: 3D picture of both the parts for the mould.
The mould is produced in a simple way to keep the expenses low. The upper part is a plate which
is bend two times, with dimensions, as shown in Figure 4.55 a. The lower part is reusable and made
magnetic, it is shaped in a L-shape with four small support plates at the back. The dimensions
can be seen in Figure 4.55 b. When the two parts are assembled, as shown in Figure 4.55 c, the
front of the lower part is aligned with the front of the upper part, closing all openings. The holes
in the parts will form a circular hole for the reinforcement and sealing the mould.
(a) Upper part.
(b) Lower part.
(c) Assembled.
Figure 4.55: Sections of the mould.
The dimensions of the mould can variate for every project, depending on the amount of reinforce-
ment and size of Ô¨Çange, however the idea is to make a standard element which can be used in most
cases. In Figure 4.56 the distances are shown, where cp is the pre-stressing wire distance from the
bottom, we is the cover layer at the sides, wb is the light concrete block width, ww is the distance
88
==PAGE:90==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
between the reinforcement lines and wc is the distance between the center lines. The distances is
changed depending on the placement of the pre-stressing wires in the cross section.
Figure 4.56: Front view of the upper part of the mould.
The connection between the mould and the reinforcement has to be done with a tight fastener
in order to fulÔ¨Ål the required casting tolerances. The connection is seen in Figure 4.57 a, where
three connections are made to hold the mould. The connection between the upper part and the
reinforcement can be done by two options, the distance tube in Figure 4.57 b or the distance screw
in Figure 4.57 c. The distance tube is placed between the reinforcement and the mould to prevent
the parts to move closer. The distance tube and the reinforcement are fastened together by a wire
going through the tube.
The distance screw is made of two parts, the screw and a cylinder. The cylinder is made with a
clamp in one end and a thread in the other end. The cylinder is placed at the reinforcement by
pressing cylinder against the reinforcement until the clamp is locked. Holes for the screw is drilled
in the mould. The screw is tightened when the mould is in place.
(a) Detail of mould attachment.
(b) Distance tube.
(c) Distance screw.
Figure 4.57: Detail of connection between mould and rebar.
89
==PAGE:91==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
5
Conclusion
The Ô¨Çanges of the Gerber-girder and the SL-decks has been dimensioned and reinforced in order
for the blade connection to work. The blade connection can not transfer moment, but works as
a simple support. The blade connection is veriÔ¨Åed and it is concluded that the beams are not a
necessity. The super-light multi-story car park can thereby be constructed based on the analysis
made in this project. The blade connection has resulted in a Ô¨Çoor height of 2.65 m, which results
in 9 Ô¨Çoors in the super-light multi-story car park with a total height of 23.85 m, which is one more
Ô¨Çoor than the multi-story car park at Bispebjerg Hospital which have a total height of 24 m. The
super-light multi-story car park can thereby be concluded more eÔ¨Äective in terms of Ô¨Çoor area.
The maximum span lengths of the SL-decks in the super-light multi-story car park is 11.7 m. This
results in an eigenfrequency of approximately 4 Hz if two cars are included in the mass and 4.62
Hz if no cars are included. This is assumed suÔ¨Écient since the building are not occupying people
for longer periods of time. If the building were to be used as an oÔ¨Éce building, a minimum of 5
Hz would have been required and shorter spans or dampers should be used. The maximum span
length can arguably limit the Ô¨Çexibility of the Gerber-system. In order to document acceptable
frequencies for long spans with the assumption of the blade connection being simply supported,
full scale tests needs to be made. Dampers could be used or the Gerber-girder could be made
wider to reduce the span length of the SL-deck.
The super-light multi-story car park is resistant to a 120 minute standard Ô¨Åre, since the deck
elements, columns and walls have been proofed to be able to withstand a 120 minute standard
Ô¨Åre. A fully developed Ô¨Åre with a Ô¨Åre load of 300 MJ/m2 has also been investigated where the
construction elements could withstand the hot condition with the exception of the Ô¨Çoating decks
and the SL-decks in the roof.
In order to simplify and make the production of the concrete elements with Ô¨Çanges economic, a
new mould was designed. The mould has been designed to be easy to apply to reduce work time
needed. One part of the mould has been made reusable while the other is permanently attached
to the decks, and therefore has to be remade which is a small expense.
90
==PAGE:92==
Technical University of Denmark
s112846 & s112880
6
Future Work
In order for the Gerber-girder made of solid concrete to go into production, both the mould and
the Gerber-girder has to be full scale tested, to see if the calculated capacities are reliable. The
testing of the Gerber-girder would mainly be focused on the Ô¨Çanges and the abilities of the blade
connection. The testing of the mould is done in order to see if the mould works as intended. It
is also tested to see if it proofs to be easier for the worker to use during production, compared to
the current methods in order to save work time and thereby make the production cheaper.
The Gerber-girder is made with the presented dimensions as standard dimensions for it to Ô¨Åt
the mould and be consistent through the building. However, the Gerber-girder could be made in
a super light version, with a standard list of dimensions in order to vary the standard version.
The varying standard dimensions could be the width and height of the deck. However the Ô¨Çange
dimensions would remain the standard dimensions, as half the height of the deck. The beneÔ¨Åts of
having a super-light Gerber-girder would be the decreased weight and the possible Ô¨Åre insulation
that the light aggregate concrete provides. A super-light version of the Gerber-girder would have
to be able to handle the shear and possible torsion acting in the deck, therefore the required
reinforcement needs space. The light aggregate blocks have to be placed with stirrups in mind.
The SL-decks spanning between the Gerber-girder could be full scale tested for vibrations. This
would have to be done with more than one SL-deck spanning between two Gerber-girders. The
full scale tested eigenfrequency of the SL-decks spanning between the Gerber-girders could serve
as documentation for longer spans, or for spans being allowable in other kinds of buildings which
occupy people, and therefore has a higher frequency requirement.
The Gerber-girder system could be used in various kinds of construction work, for example oÔ¨Éce
buildings, apartment complexes and hotels. These kinds of buildings have ventilation, insulation
and so forth, which needs space to be installed in the ceiling of every Ô¨Çoor. This can be a problem
if the buildings are made traditionally since the beams and decks will span perpendicular to each
other.
Therefore, the beams are often in the way.
The Gerber-girder system is a solution to
that since no beams are used, the construction height is reduced, which results in space for the
ventilation and insulation in the ceiling. This will make the cooperation between the construction
engineer and ventilation engineer easier and with less compromises.

‚âî

0 kN
The normal force NL is considered to act in compression in the middle of the slab H/2 from top.
The program need a guess for the depth x of the neutral axis in order to solve 
the set of equations finding the ultimative positive and negative moments. 
In case you get red marks and no result, you may change the guess value.
Recommended values are H/16 for xpos and H/4 for xneg.

transfer the wind shear to the core, if the compression works perpendicular to the deck edges. If 
the compression force works with an angle, the compression is taken as two composants. One 
as tension in the interface between the decks, the other as a compression perpendicular on the 
tension force.
